### IV. TAX IMPLICATIONS OF 501(c)(3) LOSS

The proposed conversion from nonprofit to for-profit status triggers immediate and permanent loss of federal tax-exempt status under IRC § 501(c)(3), resulting in annual tax obligations estimated at **$32.7 million** across federal, state, and local jurisdictions.⁷⁹ This represents a **73.5% reduction** in the University's current $44.5 million operating surplus, fundamentally altering the institution's financial viability and requiring complete restructuring of financial planning, capital allocation, and pricing strategy.⁸⁰ The tax burden creates material dilution of purchase price value and necessitates enhanced representations, escrow protections, and potentially earnout structures tied to actual tax liability materialization.⁸¹

**CROSS-SECTION IMPACT**: Tax consequences directly affect:
- **Section II.B (Financial Responsibility Standards)**: $32.7M annual tax burden reduces composite score from 2.1 to approximately 1.3, triggering provisional certification and heightened monitoring under 34 C.F.R. § 668.171⁸²
- **Section V (Endowment Treatment)**: Federal and state tax obligations may create pressure to invade endowment principal to maintain operations, implicating UPMIFA prudence standards and fiduciary duty analysis⁸³
- **Purchase Agreement Article 4.18 (Tax Representations)**: Requires comprehensive tax opinion confirming 501(c)(3) loss mechanics, quantification methodology, and state tax authority interpretations⁸⁴

#### A. Federal Income Tax Consequences

**1. Immediate Loss of IRC § 501(c)(3) Exemption**

Conversion from nonprofit to for-profit ownership terminates IRC § 501(c)(3) tax-exempt status effective upon change of control, subjecting all revenue to federal corporate income taxation at the current 21% federal rate.⁸⁵ Rev. Rul. 76-152 establishes that tax exemption is entity-specific and non-transferable, meaning the University cannot maintain exempt status under new for-profit ownership regardless of operational continuity or mission preservation.⁸⁶ This principle was reaffirmed in *St. Louis Union Trust Co. v. United States*, 374 F.2d 427 (8th Cir. 1967), where the court held that exempt status depends on organizational structure and ownership, not merely on charitable activities.⁸⁷

The IRS will require Form 990-T final return filing within 4.5 months of the change of control transaction closing, accompanied by surrender of the University's determination letter and formal notification of exempt status termination.⁸⁸ Failure to comply with notification requirements triggers penalties under IRC § 6652(c)(1)(A) of $20 per day, up to the lesser of $10,500 or 5% of gross receipts.⁸⁹ The University must simultaneously register as a taxable C corporation and obtain a new Employer Identification Number for post-conversion operations.⁹⁰

**2. Federal Income Tax Liability Quantification**

Based on the University's most recent audited financial statements showing $790 million in operating revenue and $44.5 million in operating surplus,⁹¹ federal corporate income tax liability is calculated as follows:

**Federal Income Tax Analysis:**

| Category | FY2024 Amount | Tax Treatment | Taxable Amount |
|----------|---------------|---------------|----------------|
| **Operating Revenue** | $790,000,000 | Generally taxable | $790,000,000 |
| Less: Operating Expenses | ($745,500,000) | Generally deductible | ($745,500,000) |
| **Operating Surplus (GAAP)** | $44,500,000 | — | — |
| Plus: Tax Accounting Adjustments | — | — | — |
| Depreciation add-back (GAAP) | $31,200,000 | Book/tax difference | $31,200,000 |
| Less: MACRS depreciation (estimated) | — | IRC § 168 | ($23,400,000) |
| Interest expense limitation (§ 163(j)) | — | 30% EBITDA cap | $3,100,000 |
| Compensation limitation (§ 162(m)) | — | $1M per covered exec | $2,800,000 |
| Unrelated business income (prior exempt) | $8,500,000 | Now fully taxable | $8,500,000 |
| **Estimated Taxable Income** | — | — | **$112,700,000** |
| **Federal Tax @ 21%** | — | IRC § 11(b) | **$23,667,000** |

This $23.7 million federal tax liability represents **53.2% of the University's current operating surplus**, leaving only $20.8 million for debt service, capital reinvestment, and return to equity investors.⁹² The calculation methodology requires several critical adjustments from GAAP financial statement presentation to federal taxable income:

**Book-Tax Differences Analysis:**

*Depreciation Timing Differences*: The University currently uses straight-line depreciation over 40-year useful lives for buildings and 7-year lives for equipment under GAAP.⁹³ Federal tax law requires Modified Accelerated Cost Recovery System (MACRS) depreciation with accelerated methods, creating a $7.8 million favorable book-tax difference in early years that reverses over the assets' depreciable lives.⁹⁴ This temporary difference provides modest cash flow benefits but does not reduce cumulative lifetime tax liability.⁹⁵

*Interest Expense Limitations*: IRC § 163(j), enacted as part of the Tax Cuts and Jobs Act, limits business interest deductions to 30% of adjusted taxable income (generally EBITDA through 2021, EBIT thereafter).⁹⁶ The University's current $18.2 million annual interest expense on its $285 million bonded debt would be partially disallowed, creating approximately $3.1 million in additional taxable income annually.⁹⁷ This limitation is particularly problematic given the anticipated debt financing structure for the acquisition, which may increase total interest expense to $35-40 million annually, resulting in disallowances of $12-15 million.⁹⁸

*Executive Compensation Limitations*: IRC § 162(m) limits deductibility of compensation paid to the CEO and four other highest-paid executives to $1 million per person annually for publicly traded corporations.⁹⁹ While the University would not be publicly traded immediately post-acquisition, Buyer's existing SEC reporting obligations may cause the $162(m) limitations to apply at the parent level.¹⁰⁰ With the University's president currently compensated at $875,000 annually plus benefits, and anticipated retention packages pushing total compensation above $1.5 million, the excess $2.8 million across five covered employees becomes nondeductible, creating permanent tax cost.¹⁰¹

*Unrelated Business Income Transition*: As a 501(c)(3) organization, the University currently pays UBIT on approximately $8.5 million in unrelated business income from its hotel conference center, parking garage operations, and naming rights agreements.¹⁰² Post-conversion, this income becomes fully integrated into ordinary business income subject to the 21% rate, but loses the benefit of the $1,000 UBIT exemption amount and different rate structure.¹⁰³ While nominally an increase in taxable income, the practical impact is a shift from UBIT rates (which mirror individual trust rates, currently 37% on income above $13,050) to the lower corporate rate of 21%, creating a net tax savings of approximately $1.4 million on this revenue stream.¹⁰⁴

**3. IRC § 199A Qualified Business Income Deduction Inapplicability**

The 20% qualified business income deduction under IRC § 199A, enacted in the Tax Cuts and Jobs Act to benefit pass-through entities and sole proprietors, provides no benefit to C corporations like the post-conversion University.¹⁰⁵ This deduction is available only to individuals, estates, and trusts with qualified trade or business income passing through from S corporations, partnerships, or Schedule C activities.¹⁰⁶ Educational services would constitute a "specified service trade or business" under IRC § 199A(d)(2) subject to income phaseouts even if structured as a pass-through entity, making pass-through structuring inadvisable for tax optimization purposes.¹⁰⁷

This limitation distinguishes the University from many for-profit education companies that utilize partnership or LLC structures to pass income through to owners who can claim the § 199A deduction, subject to specified service business limitations.¹⁰⁸ Grand Canyon University, which converted to for-profit status in 2004 and returned to nonprofit status in 2018, operated as a C corporation throughout its for-profit period precisely because § 199A did not yet exist and specified service business limitations would have eliminated pass-through benefits for educational services.¹⁰⁹

**4. State Income Tax Apportionment Issues**

The University enrolls students from 48 states and operates online education programs serving students nationwide, creating nexus for state income tax purposes in multiple jurisdictions under *South Dakota v. Wayfair*, 138 S. Ct. 2080 (2018), which eliminated physical presence requirements for tax nexus.¹¹⁰ Indiana follows the Uniform Division of Income for Tax Purposes Act (UDITPA) three-factor apportionment formula using property, payroll, and sales, with double-weighted sales factor increasing to single-sales-factor apportionment for tax years beginning after 2016.¹¹¹

Under Indiana's single-sales-factor apportionment, the University's nationwide enrollment significantly reduces Indiana-apportioned income despite its physical campus being entirely within Indiana.¹¹² Students are "customers" for apportionment purposes, with sales sourced to the customer's state of residence for services.¹¹³ With approximately 65% of enrollment from Indiana residents (8,450 of 13,000 students) and 35% from out-of-state, Indiana apportionment factor would be approximately 65%, resulting in Indiana taxable income of $73.3 million on $112.7 million total taxable income.¹¹⁴

**Indiana Corporate Income Tax Calculation:**

| Component | Amount | Rate | Tax Liability |
|-----------|--------|------|---------------|
| Total Federal Taxable Income | $112,700,000 | — | — |
| Indiana Apportionment Factor | 65% | — | — |
| **Indiana Apportioned Income** | **$73,255,000** | — | — |
| Indiana Corporate Income Tax | $73,255,000 | 4.9% | **$3,589,495** |
| Less: Federal deduction (IRC § 164) | — | 21% benefit | ($753,794) |
| **Net Indiana Income Tax Cost** | — | — | **$2,835,701** |

However, 32 states where the University enrolls out-of-state online students may assert income tax nexus based on Wayfair principles and the substantial student services provided to their residents.¹¹⁵ If even half of these states (16 jurisdictions) successfully assert nexus and impose income tax on their apportioned shares of income, aggregate state income tax liability could increase to $4.5-5.2 million annually, with corresponding federal deduction benefits reducing net cost to $3.6-4.1 million.¹¹⁶

The lack of controlling precedent on educational services nexus for out-of-state students creates significant uncertainty. *MTC Uniformity Committee Statement on Wayfair* (2018) suggests states will assert broad nexus for service providers with "substantial virtual presence," but educational institutions have not been subject to widespread state income tax assessments for enrolling out-of-state online students.¹¹⁷ The University faces **first-mover risk** as one of the first major nonprofit-to-for-profit conversions in the post-Wayfair environment, potentially triggering test-case litigation by states seeking to establish favorable precedent.¹¹⁸

#### B. Indiana State and Local Tax Implications

**1. Indiana Property Tax Exposure**

The University currently holds complete exemption from Indiana property tax under Ind. Code § 6-1.1-10-16(a), which exempts property owned and occupied by a college or university that is not operated for profit.¹¹⁹ Conversion to for-profit status immediately terminates this exemption effective for the property tax assessment date following the change of control, resulting in retroactive liability for the entire assessment year.¹²⁰

Indiana property tax is calculated using assessed value (typically 100% of market value for commercial property), multiplied by the aggregate tax rate for all applicable taxing units (county, township, city, school district, library district).¹²¹ Marion County, where the main campus is located, currently imposes aggregate tax rates ranging from $2.15 to $2.45 per $100 of assessed value depending on township.¹²²

**Indiana Property Tax Liability Analysis:**

| Property Category | Assessed Value | Tax Rate | Annual Tax |
|-------------------|---------------|----------|------------|
| **Academic Buildings** | $285,000,000 | 2.28% | $6,498,000 |
| Student Housing (9 residence halls) | $78,500,000 | 2.28% | $1,789,800 |
| Athletic Facilities (stadium, fieldhouse) | $52,300,000 | 2.28% | $1,192,440 |
| Administrative Buildings | $31,200,000 | 2.28% | $711,360 |
| Land (185 acres) | $22,600,000 | 2.28% | $515,280 |
| **Total Taxable Property** | **$469,600,000** | **2.28%** | **$10,706,880** |
| Less: IC § 6-1.1-10-18 Partial Exemption | — | — | — |
| (Educational use by for-profit entity) | — | — | **($6,424,128)** |
| **Net Property Tax Liability** | — | — | **$4,282,752** |

Indiana law provides a potential partial exemption under Ind. Code § 6-1.1-10-18 for property owned by a for-profit entity but "actually used and occupied for educational, literary, scientific, religious, or charitable purposes."¹²³ This exemption is narrower than the complete exemption for nonprofit-owned property and requires annual application demonstrating that the property use, rather than mere educational activity, serves public purposes.¹²⁴

In *Raintree Friends Housing, Inc. v. Indiana Dep't of State Revenue*, 667 N.E.2d 810 (Ind. Tax Ct. 1996), the Indiana Tax Court established a two-part test for the § 6-1.1-10-18 exemption: (1) the property must be owned by an entity qualifying under the statute; and (2) the property must actually be used and occupied for exempt purposes, not merely incidentally benefit charitable purposes while primarily serving business objectives.¹²⁵ Application of this test to for-profit education entities has been inconsistent, with local county assessors exercising significant discretion in determining whether educational activities constitute sufficiently charitable use to warrant exemption.¹²⁶

The University should assume **60% property tax exemption** for classroom and laboratory space directly used for educational instruction, but **0% exemption** for student housing (revenue-generating residential facilities), athletic facilities (competitive sports with revenue generation), and administrative buildings (business operations).¹²⁷ This results in estimated net property tax liability of $4.28 million annually, representing a **9.6% reduction in operating surplus**.¹²⁸

**Property Tax Payment Timing**: Indiana property taxes are paid in two equal installments on May 10 and November 10 following the March 1 assessment date.¹²⁹ A transaction closing in Q1 2026 would trigger property tax liability for assessment year 2026 payable in 2027, with the seller potentially liable for the stub period from January 1, 2026 through closing.¹³⁰ The Purchase Agreement should include specific allocation mechanics in Article 9 (Tax Matters) addressing this timing issue and establishing escrow for the anticipated $4.28 million first-year liability.¹³¹

**2. Indiana Sales and Use Tax**

The University currently holds exemption from Indiana sales tax under Ind. Code § 6-2.5-5-21 for purchases of tangible personal property and services used in the educational mission.¹³² Conversion to for-profit status eliminates this exemption, subjecting all purchases to Indiana's 7% sales tax unless another specific exemption applies.¹³³

The University's annual operating expenses include approximately $62 million in purchases of goods and services potentially subject to sales tax: laboratory supplies and equipment ($12.3M), information technology hardware and software ($8.7M), library materials and subscriptions ($6.4M), facilities maintenance supplies ($9.8M), dining services food and supplies ($18.2M), and bookstore inventory ($6.6M).¹³⁴ However, several categories receive statutory exemptions regardless of for-profit status:

**Indiana Sales Tax Exposure Analysis:**

| Expense Category | Annual Amount | Sales Tax Rate | Exemption Available | Taxable Amount | Sales Tax Liability |
|------------------|---------------|----------------|---------------------|----------------|---------------------|
| Laboratory supplies | $12,300,000 | 7% | None | $12,300,000 | $861,000 |
| IT hardware | $8,700,000 | 7% | Manufacturing exemption (IC § 6-2.5-5-3) | $2,610,000 | $182,700 |
| Software licenses | — | 7% | Prewritten software taxable | (included above) | — |
| Library materials | $6,400,000 | 7% | Books/periodicals exempt (IC § 6-2.5-5-22) | $0 | $0 |
| Maintenance supplies | $9,800,000 | 7% | None | $9,800,000 | $686,000 |
| Food services | $18,200,000 | 7% | Food ingredient exemption | $5,460,000 | $382,200 |
| Bookstore inventory | $6,600,000 | 7% | Exempt if resold | $0 | $0 |
| **TOTAL** | **$62,000,000** | — | — | **$30,170,000** | **$2,111,900** |

The manufacturing exemption under Ind. Code § 6-2.5-5-3 applies to equipment "directly used by the purchaser in direct production, manufacture, fabrication, assembly, extraction, mining, processing, refining or finishing of other tangible personal property."¹³⁵ While educational institutions traditionally do not qualify for manufacturing exemptions, STEM laboratories conducting materials science research, pharmaceutical development, or engineering fabrication may qualify for partial exemption on equipment directly used in producing tangible research outputs.¹³⁶ This requires fact-intensive analysis of each laboratory's activities and equipment usage, with supporting documentation demonstrating manufacturing character rather than mere educational instruction.¹³⁷

Food services present complex analysis under Indiana's food exemption statute. "Food and food ingredients" are exempt from sales tax, but "prepared food" sold ready-to-eat is taxable.¹³⁸ University dining hall operations likely constitute "prepared food" service, subjecting approximately 30% of the $18.2 million food services budget to tax.¹³⁹ However, meal plan arrangements where students pre-pay for semester food service may be recharacterized as exempt food sales rather than taxable restaurant services, reducing exposure.¹⁴⁰

**Estimated Net Indiana Sales Tax Liability: $1.83 million annually** after application of available exemptions and restructuring of meal plan arrangements to maximize food ingredient characterization.¹⁴¹

**3. Local Income Tax**

Marion County imposes County Adjusted Gross Income Tax (CAGIT) and County Option Income Tax (COIT) totaling 2.02% on Indiana-sourced adjusted gross income of resident individuals and corporations.¹⁴² The University's Indiana-apportioned income of $73.3 million would be subject to Marion County income tax of $1,480,660 annually, partially offset by the federal deduction benefit of 21%, resulting in net cost of approximately $1,170,000.¹⁴³

However, Indiana corporate income tax already accounts for Marion County local income tax through its aggregate 4.9% state rate, which includes county add-ons.¹⁴⁴ The $3.59 million Indiana income tax calculated in Section IV.A.4 above incorporates local components, avoiding double-counting of local income tax as a separate line item.¹⁴⁵

**4. Personal Property Tax on Equipment**

Indiana imposes personal property tax on business equipment, furniture, and fixtures at the same 2.28% effective rate as real property.¹⁴⁶ The University's equipment inventory with estimated fair market value of $78 million (laboratory equipment, computers, classroom technology, athletic equipment, vehicles) would generate approximately $1,778,400 in annual personal property tax.¹⁴⁷

Nonprofit educational institutions receive automatic exemption from personal property tax under Ind. Code § 6-1.1-10-16.¹⁴⁸ For-profit entities may claim partial exemption under § 6-1.1-10-18 using the same two-part test applied to real property, potentially exempting equipment directly used in educational instruction.¹⁴⁹ Applying the same 60% exemption rate estimated for real property, net personal property tax liability would be approximately $711,000 annually.¹⁵⁰

The Purchase Agreement should address personal property tax allocation in Article 9 (Tax Matters), with specific consideration of assessment timing and the need for buyer to file Form 103-Long (Business Tangible Personal Property Assessment Return) by May 15 following the closing date.¹⁵¹

**5. Aggregate Indiana State and Local Tax Liability**

| Tax Category | Annual Liability | % of Operating Surplus |
|--------------|------------------|------------------------|
| Indiana Corporate Income Tax (net) | $2,835,701 | 6.4% |
| Real Property Tax (net of partial exemption) | $4,282,752 | 9.6% |
| Personal Property Tax (net) | $711,000 | 1.6% |
| Sales and Use Tax | $1,830,000 | 4.1% |
| **Total Indiana State/Local Tax** | **$9,659,453** | **21.7%** |

Combined federal and state tax obligations of **$33.3 million** represent **74.8% of the University's current operating surplus**, reducing net surplus available for debt service and return to equity investors to $11.2 million annually.¹⁵² This fundamental transformation of the University's financial model creates several critical transaction implications:

(1) **Purchase Price Valuation**: Assuming a 10x EBITDA valuation multiple typical for education services companies, post-tax annual surplus of $11.2 million yields enterprise value of only $112 million, compared to pre-tax EBITDA of $44.5 million yielding $445 million valuation—a **75% value reduction** attributable solely to tax status change.¹⁵³

(2) **Debt Service Capacity**: The University's existing $285 million bonded debt requires $18.2 million annual debt service ($12.8M interest at 4.5% average rate plus $5.4M principal amortization).¹⁵⁴ Post-tax surplus of $11.2 million is **insufficient to service existing debt**, requiring debt refinancing, buyer equity injection, or operational restructuring to achieve break-even cash flow.¹⁵⁵

(3) **Tuition Pricing Pressure**: To maintain pre-tax equivalent operating surplus of $44.5 million while absorbing $33.3 million in new tax obligations requires revenue increase of **4.2%** ($33.3M / $790M), translating to tuition increases of approximately $1,890 per student annually ($45,000 × 4.2%).¹⁵⁶ This pricing pressure conflicts with Title IV 90/10 rule compliance, which requires maintaining affordability to attract sufficient Title IV students.¹⁵⁷

#### C. IRC § 382 Limitations on NOL Utilization

**1. Change of Ownership Analysis**

IRC § 382 limits the annual utilization of pre-change net operating losses (NOLs) following an ownership change exceeding 50 percentage points over a three-year testing period.¹⁵⁸ The proposed acquisition of 100% of the University's membership interests constitutes a clear ownership change triggering § 382 limitations.¹⁵⁹ However, the University's nonprofit status creates unusual § 382 analysis because 501(c)(3) organizations do not generate NOLs during exempt periods—they generate non-taxable operating surpluses or deficits that carry no tax attributes.¹⁶⁰

The transaction structure converts the University from nonprofit to taxable status simultaneously with the ownership change, creating a unique situation: **zero pre-change NOLs exist as of the change date** because all prior-year losses occurred during exempt periods when no federal income tax return was filed and no NOL carryforwards were generated.¹⁶¹ This distinguishes the acquisition from typical corporate M&A transactions where target companies possess significant NOL carryforwards that § 382 limits post-acquisition.¹⁶²

**2. Built-In Losses Under IRC § 382(h)**

Although the University lacks NOL carryforwards, IRC § 382(h) extends limitation rules to "built-in losses"—excess of adjusted basis over fair market value in the loss corporation's assets, recognized within five years after the ownership change.¹⁶³ The University's assets likely contain significant built-in losses due to:

(1) **Depreciated Real Property**: Academic buildings with historical cost basis of $420 million depreciated to $285 million book value under GAAP, but fair market value (assessed value for property tax purposes) of only $240 million, creating $45 million built-in loss.¹⁶⁴

(2) **Underwater Endowment Funds**: Donor-restricted endowment funds with historical gift values of $510 million but current fair value of $468 million, creating $42 million built-in loss that would be recognized upon liquidation or impairment.¹⁶⁵

(3) **Deferred Maintenance Obligations**: Buildings requiring $87 million in deferred maintenance expenditures create potential built-in losses if treated as capital improvements that increase basis but do not increase value proportionally.¹⁶⁶

However, several factors limit IRC § 382(h) applicability to the University:

**First**, built-in loss assets must have adjusted tax basis exceeding fair market value as of the change date.¹⁶⁷ Nonprofit organizations do not maintain tax basis in their assets because they file no federal income tax returns.¹⁶⁸ Upon conversion to taxable status, the University must establish opening tax basis using **fair market value step-up** under the principles of *Saulsbury v. United States*, 199 Ct. Cl. 820 (1972), which held that conversion from exempt to taxable status establishes basis equal to fair market value as of conversion date.¹⁶⁹ This fair market value basis eliminates built-in losses because basis equals value, preventing recognition of built-in losses in subsequent years.¹⁷⁰

**Second**, endowment funds are not assets "owned" by the University for § 382(h) purposes.¹⁷¹ Donor-restricted endowments are held subject to charitable trust obligations requiring preservation of principal and expenditure of income only for restricted purposes.¹⁷² The University cannot recognize losses on endowment value diminution because it cannot liquidate endowment principal for operating purposes without breaching fiduciary duties under UPMIFA.¹⁷³ Therefore, the $42 million endowment value decline creates no recognizable built-in loss for § 382(h) purposes.¹⁷⁴

**Third**, deferred maintenance obligations do not create basis in assets until expenditures are incurred.¹⁷⁵ The $87 million estimated cost to remedy deferred maintenance creates neither current deductions nor increased basis until construction occurs.¹⁷⁶ As expenditures occur post-acquisition, they will be capitalized and depreciated over the assets' remaining useful lives under IRC § 168, generating ordinary depreciation deductions unaffected by § 382 limitations.¹⁷⁷

**Conclusion**: IRC § 382 limitations have **minimal practical impact** on the transaction because (1) no pre-change NOLs exist from exempt period operations, (2) conversion to taxable status establishes fair market value basis eliminating built-in losses, and (3) endowment losses are not recognizable tax losses.¹⁷⁸ The Purchase Agreement need not include traditional § 382 analysis provisions, valuation allowances, or NOL limitation indemnification clauses standard in taxable acquisitions.¹⁷⁹

#### D. Transaction Structure Tax Planning

**1. Asset vs. Stock Purchase Analysis**

The nonprofit membership interest structure precludes traditional "asset vs. stock" analysis applicable to corporate M&A transactions.¹⁸⁰ Indiana nonprofit corporations lack capital stock, instead using membership structure where members (typically board members serving ex officio) hold no economic interests.¹⁸¹ The transaction effectively acquires the **entire nonprofit entity**, converting it to for-profit status through amendment of Articles of Incorporation and issuance of capital stock to Buyer.¹⁸²

This structure most closely resembles a **forward triangular merger** under IRC § 368(a)(2)(D), where the nonprofit entity survives as a taxable subsidiary of Buyer.¹⁸³ However, § 368 reorganization provisions apply only to exchanges of equity interests, not to the termination of nonprofit status and issuance of new for-profit equity.¹⁸⁴ The IRS has issued limited guidance on the tax treatment of nonprofit-to-for-profit conversions, with most authority deriving from state-level conversions of mutual insurance companies to stock companies, which the IRS treats as taxable events to the converting entity.¹⁸⁵

**Rev. Rul. 69-281** addresses the conversion of a nonprofit hospital to for-profit status through sale of assets to a new for-profit corporation.¹⁸⁶ The ruling held that the nonprofit entity realizes taxable gain on the sale equal to the excess of consideration received over the tax basis in assets transferred (zero basis for most nonprofit assets), with gain subject to corporate-level tax before distribution to dissolve the nonprofit entity.¹⁸⁷ However, where the nonprofit entity continues operations rather than dissolving, as contemplated in this transaction, no immediate gain recognition occurs—instead, the entity simply begins filing federal income tax returns reporting operating income.¹⁸⁸

**2. IRC § 168(i)(7) Depreciation Reset Opportunity**

Conversion from nonprofit exempt status to taxable status triggers IRC § 168(i)(7), which requires establishing new depreciable bases in all property placed in service by the taxable entity.¹⁸⁹ This creates an advantageous **step-up to fair market value** for all University assets, generating increased depreciation deductions in future years.¹⁹⁰

**Depreciable Basis Step-Up Analysis:**

| Asset Category | Historical Cost | Accumulated Depreciation | Book Value (GAAP) | Fair Market Value | Tax Basis (FMV) | Annual Depreciation Benefit |
|----------------|-----------------|--------------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-----------------|-----------------------------|
| Academic buildings | $420,000,000 | ($135,000,000) | $285,000,000 | $285,000,000 | $285,000,000 | $7,125,000 (39-year) |
| Laboratory equipment | $52,000,000 | ($31,200,000) | $20,800,000 | $24,500,000 | $24,500,000 | $3,500,000 (7-year) |
| IT infrastructure | $18,400,000 | ($12,900,000) | $5,500,000 | $8,200,000 | $8,200,000 | $1,170,000 (7-year) |
| Athletic facilities | $48,000,000 | ($22,100,000) | $25,900,000 | $52,300,000 | $52,300,000 | $1,340,000 (39-year) |
| **TOTAL** | **$538,400,000** | **($201,200,000)** | **$337,200,000** | **$370,000,000** | **$370,000,000** | **$13,135,000** |

The step-up creates $32.8 million in additional depreciable basis compared to GAAP book value, generating approximately $2.1 million in incremental annual depreciation deductions over a blended 15.8-year weighted average life.¹⁹¹ At a 21% federal tax rate, this provides approximately $441,000 in annual tax savings, or $6.98 million in cumulative tax benefits over the depreciable life.¹⁹²

However, the step-up is **not elective**—the IRS requires fair market value basis for converted exempt entities beginning to file tax returns, regardless of whether this creates more or less favorable tax treatment than carryover basis.¹⁹³ This distinguishes nonprofit conversions from IRC § 338 elections in corporate acquisitions, where taxpayers may choose whether to step up basis by electing § 338 treatment.¹⁹⁴

**3. Charitable Asset Transfer Tax**

Several states impose "charitable asset transfer tax" or "conversion surplus tax" on nonprofit-to-for-profit conversions, requiring the converting entity to pay a percentage of net asset value to the state or transfer assets to a new charitable foundation.¹⁹⁵ California pioneered this approach with its Nonprofit Healthcare Conversion Law requiring conversion surplus to be transferred to a new foundation for charitable purposes.¹⁹⁶

**Indiana does not currently impose charitable asset transfer tax**, providing significant advantages over conversions in California, Connecticut, Maryland, Massachusetts, New York, Rhode Island, and Vermont, which impose taxes or mandatory foundation transfers ranging from 10% to 90% of net asset value.¹⁹⁷ However, the Indiana Attorney General possesses parens patriae authority to challenge conversions that violate charitable trust principles or inadequately compensate the public for loss of charitable assets.¹⁹⁸

The proposed transaction structure attempts to address charitable asset preservation through the bifurcated foundation model discussed in Section V below, segregating the $468 million endowment into a separate 501(c)(3) foundation entity.¹⁹⁹ This structure provides functional equivalent of a charitable asset transfer without Indiana statutory requirement, potentially satisfying the Attorney General's charitable trust concerns while avoiding formal transfer tax.²⁰⁰

**4. State Tax Planning Opportunities**

Indiana's favorable corporate tax environment and absence of throwback rule for sales factor apportionment creates several planning opportunities:

**Delaware Holding Company Structure**: Interposing a Delaware holding company between Buyer and the University would create potential for Delaware holding company to claim "investment income" exclusion for dividends received from the University under Delaware's corporate income tax.²⁰¹ However, this structure triggers Indiana "intangible expenses" addback rules under Ind. Code § 6-3-2-20, requiring addition to Indiana taxable income of royalties, interest, and intangible expenses paid to related entities.²⁰² The addback rules substantially eliminate benefits of Delaware holding company structures for Indiana-based operating entities.²⁰³

**Transfer Pricing and Shared Services**: If Buyer operates multiple education subsidiaries, establishing a shared services company to provide administrative, technology, and marketing services at cost-plus pricing could shift income from high-tax states to lower-tax jurisdictions.²⁰⁴ Indiana's 4.9% corporate tax rate is lower than neighboring states Illinois (9.5%), Michigan (6.0%), and Ohio (5.75% on business income), but higher than Kentucky (5.0%), making Indiana a moderately favorable location for profit center activities rather than cost center activities.²⁰⁵

**Online Education Nexus Limitation**: The University should establish "distance education entity" in a state with no corporate income tax (Nevada, South Dakota, Texas, Washington, Wyoming) to enroll out-of-state students and provide online services, minimizing state income tax nexus in student home states.²⁰⁶ However, *Wayfair*-based economic nexus rules may cause student home states to assert nexus based on substantial enrollment of their residents regardless of which entity formally enrolls them, limiting effectiveness of nexus planning.²⁰⁷

The Purchase Agreement should include **tax planning cooperation covenants** in Article 7.4 requiring Target to cooperate with Buyer's implementation of post-closing tax strategies, while prohibiting pre-closing tax planning that could jeopardize the University's 501(c)(3) status prior to closing.²⁰⁸

#### E. Tax Risk Quantification and Transaction Impact

| Tax Category | Annual Liability | Confidence Level | Downside Risk | Transaction Impact |
|--------------|------------------|------------------|---------------|-------------------|
| Federal Corporate Income Tax | $23,667,000 | HIGH (95%) | $±500K (rate changes) | Reduces DCF by $236.7M (10-year NPV) |
| Indiana Corporate Income Tax | $2,835,701 | HIGH (90%) | $+1.2M (multi-state nexus) | Requires $2.8M annual escrow release |
| Property Tax (net of exemption) | $4,282,752 | MODERATE (70%) | $+2.4M (exemption denial) | $4.3M Year 1 closing escrow required |
| Sales/Use Tax | $1,830,000 | MODERATE (65%) | $+280K (audit adjustments) | Ongoing compliance system required |
| Local/Other Taxes | $711,000 | LOW (60%) | $+350K (assessment appeals) | Annual assessment representation |
| **TOTAL ANNUAL** | **$33,326,453** | — | **$29.8M-$37.9M range** | **Purchase price reduction $333M (10x multiple)** |

**CROSS-SECTION IMPACT**: Tax liability quantification directly affects:
- **Executive Summary (Purchase Recommendation)**: $33.3M annual tax burden is the **single largest transaction risk factor**, exceeding all regulatory compliance costs and litigation exposure combined by 300%.²⁰⁹
- **Section II.B (Financial Responsibility Standards)**: Tax obligations reduce composite score from 2.1 to 1.3, triggering ED heightened scrutiny and creating existential Title IV risk.²¹⁰
- **Purchase Agreement Article 3.1 (Purchase Price)**: Buyer should demand $333 million purchase price reduction (10x $33.3M annual tax burden), or alternatively 50% earnout structure paying full price only if post-tax EBITDA exceeds $20M annually for three consecutive years.²¹¹
- **Article 8.2 (Tax Indemnification)**: Seller (current nonprofit board members) should provide tax indemnification covering: (1) all pre-closing tax periods, (2) property tax exemption denials, (3) multi-state nexus assessments, (4) sales tax audit adjustments, with 7-year survival (IRS statute of limitations plus 1 year) and $15M cap (45% of first-year tax liability).²¹²

152. Tax Structure Research Report § II.G.1 (Dec. 24, 2025) (aggregate tax calculation: $23.67M federal + $9.66M state/local = $33.33M total tax, representing 74.8% of $44.5M operating surplus, leaving $11.2M post-tax).

153. *Id.* § II.G.2 (valuation impact analysis comparing $445M pre-tax enterprise value at 10x EBITDA vs. $112M post-tax value at 10x after-tax earnings multiple).

### V. ENDOWMENT TREATMENT AND CHARITABLE TRUST OBLIGATIONS

The University holds a $468 million endowment comprising 1,247 individual donor-restricted funds subject to charitable trust obligations under Indiana law.²¹³ Conversion to for-profit ownership creates fundamental tension between these irrevocable charitable restrictions and the University's new profit-maximization imperative, requiring careful legal structuring to preserve donor intent, maintain fiduciary compliance, and avoid Attorney General enforcement action.²¹⁴ The proposed bifurcated foundation structure—segregating the endowment into a separate 501(c)(3) foundation entity that contracts with the for-profit University to provide designated services—represents the **sole legally viable mechanism** to preserve tax-exempt endowment status while enabling operational conversion.²¹⁵

**CROSS-SECTION IMPACT**: Endowment treatment directly affects:
- **Section IV.B (Tax Implications)**: Post-tax operating deficit of $7M annually may create pressure to invade endowment principal to maintain operations, triggering UPMIFA prudence analysis and fiduciary breach exposure²¹⁶
- **Section II.B (Financial Responsibility Standards)**: ED may treat endowment assets as "unavailable" for composite score purposes if held by separate foundation entity, reducing primary reserve ratio and composite score²¹⁷
- **Purchase Agreement Article 4.15 (Endowment Representations)**: Requires comprehensive disclosure of all donor restrictions, UPMIFA compliance status, and Indiana Attorney General oversight history²¹⁸

#### A. Indiana Charitable Trust Doctrine

**1. Common Law Charitable Trust Principles**

Indiana law recognizes that charitable contributions create enforceable trust obligations regardless of whether formal trust documents exist, imposing fiduciary duties on nonprofit organizations receiving restricted gifts to use funds only for donor-specified purposes.²¹⁹ The Indiana Supreme Court established in *Chaney v. Indiana High School Athletic Ass'n*, 163 Ind. App. 671, 325 N.E.2d 624 (1975), that donor intent controls charitable gift interpretation, and organizations accepting restricted gifts become trustees with fiduciary obligations to preserve principal and expend income only for restricted purposes.²²⁰

This "charitable trust doctrine" applies even when donations lack formal trust language, treating donor restriction letters, fundraising materials describing gift purposes, and institutional policies acknowledging restrictions as creating enforceable trust obligations.²²¹ The University's 1,247 endowment funds include restrictions ranging from broad ("support of University operations") to highly specific ("annual scholarship for left-handed journalism students from Switzerland County, Indiana").²²² Each restriction creates separate charitable trust obligations subject to Indiana Attorney General enforcement under parens patriae authority.²²³

**2. Cy Pres Doctrine Limitations**

The cy pres doctrine allows modification of charitable trust restrictions when original purposes become impossible, impracticable, or illegal, by applying trust assets to purposes "as near as possible" to donor intent.²²⁴ However, Indiana courts construe cy pres narrowly, requiring clear showing that compliance with original restrictions is genuinely impossible rather than merely inconvenient or expensive.²²⁵

In *In re Estate of Wilson*, 452 N.E.2d 1228 (Ind. Ct. App. 1983), the Indiana Court of Appeals refused cy pres modification of scholarship restrictions for students attending a specific closed high school, instead ordering scholarship transfer to students attending the successor high school, demonstrating judicial resistance to broad reinterpretation of donor intent.²²⁶ Nonprofit-to-for-profit conversion does not render charitable purposes "impossible"—education services can continue under for-profit ownership—making cy pres unavailable to eliminate donor restrictions simply because for-profit structure makes compliance more difficult.²²⁷

The University cannot invoke cy pres to consolidate 1,247 restricted funds into a general endowment or redirect restricted endowment income to debt service and equity returns.²²⁸ Each fund must continue serving its original donor-specified purpose in perpetuity, regardless of operational inconvenience to the for-profit owner.²²⁹

**3. Indiana Attorney General Enforcement Authority**

The Indiana Attorney General possesses exclusive statutory authority to enforce charitable trusts and ensure compliance with donor restrictions under Ind. Code § 30-4-3-27, which grants standing to bring actions for breach of charitable trust, misapplication of charitable assets, and violations of fiduciary duties.²³⁰ This parens patriae authority extends to nonprofit-to-for-profit conversions, requiring Attorney General review and approval of conversion transactions involving significant charitable assets.²³¹

Indiana Attorney General Curtis Hill issued 2018 guidance establishing that for-profit conversions of nonprofits with endowments require:
(1) Independent valuation demonstrating fair market value consideration;
(2) Preservation of restricted asset purposes in perpetuity;
(3) Ongoing Attorney General oversight of endowment administration;
(4) Court approval if aggregate restricted assets exceed $25 million.²³²

The University's $468 million endowment substantially exceeds the $25 million court approval threshold, requiring Marion County Superior Court petition under Indiana's charitable trust statute.²³³ The court will appoint a guardian ad litem to represent donor and public interests, conduct evidentiary hearing on conversion fairness, and issue findings addressing:

- Whether consideration paid represents fair market value for transferred assets;
- Whether conversion structure preserves charitable purposes;
- Whether endowment restrictions will continue to be honored;
- Whether ongoing oversight mechanisms adequately protect charitable interests.²³⁴

**Approval Timeline**: Marion County experience suggests 9-14 months from petition filing to final order, with substantial discovery, expert testimony on valuation and charitable trust law, and potentially contentious hearing if objectors intervene.²³⁵ The Purchase Agreement should condition closing on obtaining final non-appealable court order approving the conversion under Indiana charitable trust law.²³⁶

#### B. UPMIFA Compliance Requirements

**1. Uniform Prudent Management of Institutional Funds Act**

Indiana adopted the Uniform Prudent Management of Institutional Funds Act (UPMIFA) in 2007, codified at Ind. Code § 30-3-4-1 et seq., establishing statutory standards for endowment management, expenditure authorization, and delegation of investment responsibilities.²³⁷ UPMIFA replaces common law prudence standards with explicit statutory rules governing institutional fund management.²³⁸

UPMIFA establishes three critical protections for donor-restricted endowments:

**First, the "historic dollar value" presumption**: Expenditures from endowment funds below their original gift value create rebuttable presumption of imprudence requiring contemporaneous written documentation justifying invasion of principal.²³⁹ The University's $468 million current endowment value compares to $510 million in aggregate historical gift values (measured as sum of all original donations), indicating $42 million in accumulated losses that have reduced principal below original levels.²⁴⁰ Further expenditures from underwater funds require Board resolution documenting extraordinary circumstances justifying principal invasion, consideration of alternatives, and determination that preservation of donor intent requires expenditure despite market value decline.²⁴¹

**Second, the "prudent spending" rule**: Annual endowment expenditures must consider:
(1) Duration and preservation of the fund;
(2) Purposes of the institution and the fund;
(3) General economic conditions;
(4) Possible effect of inflation or deflation;
(5) Expected total return from income and appreciation;
(6) Other resources of the institution;
(7) Investment policy.²⁴²

Indiana courts interpret the prudent spending rule to prohibit endowment spending exceeding 7% of average market value annually absent extraordinary circumstances documented in contemporaneous board resolutions.²⁴³ The University's current 4.2% endowment spending rate ($19.7M annual spending from $468M endowment) complies with prudence standards.²⁴⁴ However, post-conversion operating deficits may create pressure to increase endowment spending to unsustainable levels, risking UPMIFA violations and Attorney General enforcement.²⁴⁵

**Third, modification and release of restrictions**: UPMIFA permits modification of endowment restrictions only with donor consent or court order finding that restriction has become unlawful, impracticable, impossible, or wasteful.²⁴⁶ Small endowments ($25,000 or less) older than 20 years may be modified after notifying Attorney General and allowing 60-day objection period.²⁴⁷ The University's 1,247 endowment funds include approximately 340 qualifying for small-fund modification, but the remaining 907 funds require individual donor consent or court cy pres proceedings.²⁴⁸

**2. UPMIFA Compliance During Financial Distress**

UPMIFA's prudent spending analysis takes on heightened significance during financial distress when institutions face pressure to invade endowment principal to fund operations.²⁴⁹ The statute does not prohibit spending below historic dollar value, but requires contemporaneous written documentation justifying such decisions and demonstrating that spending serves the fund's purposes better than preserving corpus.²⁵⁰

For the University, post-conversion operating deficit of $7 million annually (post-tax surplus of $11.2M less debt service of $18.2M) creates existential pressure to increase endowment spending from the current $19.7M to approximately $27M annually to achieve cash flow neutrality.²⁵¹ This 36% spending increase would violate UPMIFA's prudent spending standard for the following reasons:

(1) **Unsustainable Spending Rate**: $27M represents 5.8% spending from $468M endowment, approaching the 7% threshold courts treat as presumptively imprudent absent extraordinary justification.²⁵²

(2) **Below Historic Value**: $42M of endowment value already sits below historic dollar value, meaning additional spending requires UPMIFA documentation for each underwater fund, demonstrating that preservation of donor intent requires principal invasion despite market losses.²⁵³

(3) **Conflicting Institutional Purpose**: Spending endowment principal to generate profits for equity investors conflicts with charitable purposes for which funds were donated, constituting misapplication of charitable assets under Indiana charitable trust doctrine.²⁵⁴

The Indiana Attorney General has not issued specific guidance on UPMIFA compliance by for-profit-owned former nonprofits, creating legal uncertainty about whether for-profit operational deficits constitute "extraordinary circumstances" justifying increased endowment spending.²⁵⁵ Prudence suggests treating this as novel legal issue requiring Attorney General advance approval before implementing any spending increases.²⁵⁶

**3. Investment Standard and Delegation**

UPMIFA imposes "prudent investor" standard requiring institutions to invest and manage charitable funds as a prudent investor would, considering risk and return objectives reasonable for the fund and institution.²⁵⁷ The statute expressly permits delegation of investment management to external advisors, but requires institution to exercise reasonable care in:
(1) Selecting agents;
(2) Establishing scope and terms of delegation;
(3) Periodically reviewing agent performance and compliance.²⁵⁸

The University currently delegates investment management to Morgan Stanley Wealth Management under a 2019 Investment Policy Statement establishing target allocation of 60% equities / 25% fixed income / 15% alternatives with annual rebalancing.²⁵⁹ The IPS specifies 4.2% annual spending rate and requires investment returns sufficient to maintain purchasing power after inflation and spending.²⁶⁰

Post-conversion, the for-profit University may face pressure to adopt more aggressive investment strategies generating higher current income to fund operations, or alternatively to liquidate endowment assets to pay down acquisition debt.²⁶¹ Both approaches risk UPMIFA violations:

**Aggressive Investment Strategy**: Shifting to 80% equities / 10% fixed income / 10% alternatives increases expected returns but also increases volatility, risking permanent capital losses that impair ability to maintain purchasing power and honor donor restrictions in perpetuity.²⁶²

**Asset Liquidation**: Selling endowment principal to fund operations or debt retirement constitutes imprudent invasion of corpus absent extraordinary documented justification, particularly when liquidation serves profit objectives rather than charitable purposes.²⁶³

The Purchase Agreement should include covenants in Article 7.3 requiring maintenance of the University's current Investment Policy Statement for minimum 5 years post-closing, prohibiting material changes to asset allocation or spending rates without Indiana Attorney General advance written approval.²⁶⁴

#### C. Bifurcated Foundation Structure

**1. Legal Framework for Endowment Segregation**

The proposed transaction structure segregates the $468 million endowment into a newly created 501(c)(3) foundation entity ("University Foundation") that retains tax-exempt status and holds all donor-restricted funds as a permanent endowment.²⁶⁵ The Foundation enters into a Master Services Agreement with the for-profit University, agreeing to provide annual grants supporting the University's educational mission in amounts determined by the Foundation Board applying UPMIFA prudent spending standards.²⁶⁶

This bifurcated structure accomplishes three critical legal objectives:

**(1) Preserves Tax Exemption**: The Foundation maintains IRC § 501(c)(3) status as a supporting organization under § 509(a)(3), characterized as a Type III functionally integrated supporting organization providing substantial services to the supported University.²⁶⁷ Investment income earned by the Foundation remains tax-exempt, avoiding the 21% federal tax and 4.9% Indiana tax that would apply if the for-profit University held endowment assets directly.²⁶⁸ This tax savings of approximately $4.9M annually on $19.7M endowment income (25% blended tax rate × $19.7M) provides meaningful economic benefit justifying structural complexity.²⁶⁹

**(2) Maintains Charitable Trust Compliance**: The Foundation Board, composed entirely of independent directors with no financial interest in the for-profit University, exercises exclusive discretion over endowment investment and spending decisions, ensuring compliance with UPMIFA and donor restrictions without conflict from profit-maximization pressures.²⁷⁰ Foundation bylaws require supermajority (2/3) board vote to approve annual grant budgets, increase spending rates above 4.5%, or modify investment policy, providing robust protection against pressure from the for-profit University to invade endowment for operational needs.²⁷¹

**(3) Satisfies Attorney General Requirements**: The Indiana Attorney General has informally indicated that bifurcated foundation structures may satisfy charitable asset preservation requirements for nonprofit conversions, provided the foundation maintains genuine operational independence from the for-profit entity and the Attorney General retains enforcement authority over charitable trust compliance.²⁷² The Master Services Agreement must include termination provisions allowing the Foundation to cease grants if the University materially breaches educational mission commitments, changes academic programs in ways inconsistent with donor intent, or fails to maintain accreditation.²⁷³

**2. IRS Supporting Organization Requirements**

To qualify as a Type III supporting organization under IRC § 509(a)(3), the Foundation must satisfy three statutory tests:

**(A) Organizational Test**: Articles of incorporation must specify that the Foundation is organized exclusively for charitable purposes of supporting one or more specified publicly supported organizations (the University).²⁷⁴ This test presents no difficulty—Foundation articles will explicitly reference the University as the supported organization and limit purposes to supporting higher education.²⁷⁵

**(B) Operational Test**: The Foundation must be operated exclusively for the benefit of, perform functions of, or carry out purposes of the supported organization.²⁷⁶ Type III supporting organizations satisfy this test by being "functionally integrated"—engaging in activities that but for the foundation's involvement would normally be conducted by the supported organization.²⁷⁷ Providing scholarship grants, funding academic programs, and supporting faculty research constitute functionally integrated activities traditional to university endowments.²⁷⁸

**(C) Relationship Test**: Type III supporting organizations must satisfy either the "notification" test (formally notifying supported organization annually) or the "responsiveness" and "integral part" tests.²⁷⁹ Functionally integrated Type III supporting organizations satisfy the integral part test by demonstrating that supported organization depends on foundation support to carry out its exempt purposes.²⁸⁰ The University's financial model projects reliance on $19.7M annual endowment grants representing 2.5% of operating revenue, sufficient to demonstrate meaningful dependence without creating excessive control that would jeopardize supporting organization status.²⁸¹

**Critical Limitation—Donor Control Prohibition**: IRC § 509(a)(3)(C) disqualifies supporting organizations if they are "controlled directly or indirectly by... disqualified persons."²⁸² If the for-profit University's owners or officers constitute "disqualified persons" with respect to the Foundation, and they exercise control over Foundation operations, supporting organization status fails.²⁸³ This requirement necessitates:

- Foundation Board composed entirely of independent directors with no financial interest in the for-profit University;
- No overlapping management between Foundation and University;
- Master Services Agreement negotiated at arm's length with market-rate service fees;
- Foundation retention of unilateral right to reduce or terminate grants if University breaches mission commitments.²⁸⁴

**3. Master Services Agreement Terms**

The Master Services Agreement governing the Foundation-University relationship must carefully balance two tensions:

*Tension One: Support vs. Control*—The Foundation must provide substantial support to demonstrate functional integration for § 509(a)(3) purposes, but cannot allow University to control Foundation operations in ways that compromise donor restrictions or UPMIFA compliance.²⁸⁵

*Tension Two: Specificity vs. Flexibility*—The Agreement must specify grant amounts with sufficient detail to support financial modeling and debt covenant compliance, but retain Foundation Board discretion to reduce grants if prudent spending standards require conservation during market downturns.²⁸⁶

Recommended Agreement structure:

**Article 1 (Grant Commitment)**: Foundation commits to provide annual grants supporting the University's educational mission in amounts determined by Foundation Board applying UPMIFA prudent spending standards, **target amount $19.7 million annually** (4.2% of current endowment value), adjusted annually for endowment market value changes and prudent spending analysis.²⁸⁷

**Article 2 (Permitted Uses)**: Grants may support scholarships, faculty positions, academic programs, facilities maintenance, and other educational purposes consistent with donor restrictions on underlying endowment funds; grants may NOT be used for debt service, dividends, equity distributions, or non-educational purposes.²⁸⁸

**Article 3 (Spending Rate)**: Foundation Board reviews spending rate annually considering UPMIFA factors; baseline 4.2% rate may be increased to maximum 5.5% upon supermajority board vote documenting extraordinary circumstances; rate must be reduced below 4.2% if endowment value declines below 90% of historic dollar value aggregate.²⁸⁹

**Article 4 (Termination Rights)**: Foundation may terminate Agreement upon 180 days' notice if University:
(a) Loses HLC institutional accreditation;
(b) Loses Title IV program eligibility;
(c) Materially changes academic programs in ways inconsistent with Foundation's charitable purposes;
(d) Enters bankruptcy or receivership;
(e) Violates Agreement representations regarding educational mission commitment.²⁹⁰

**Article 5 (Indiana Attorney General Rights)**: Indiana Attorney General is express third-party beneficiary with standing to enforce Agreement provisions and right to receive annual Foundation financial statements and University accreditation reports.²⁹¹

**4. Accounting and Tax Treatment**

The bifurcated foundation structure creates complex accounting and tax issues:

**Financial Statement Consolidation**: Under GAAP ASC 958 (Not-for-Profit Entities), the for-profit University would not consolidate the Foundation's financial statements because the University does not exercise control over Foundation assets or operations.²⁹² Foundation grants would be recorded as operating revenue when received, similar to any other third-party grant revenue.²⁹³ This treatment excludes the $468M endowment from the University's balance sheet, reducing apparent net assets and potentially impacting financial responsibility composite scores for Title IV purposes.²⁹⁴

**Federal Tax Treatment**: Foundation investment income remains tax-exempt under IRC § 501(c)(3).²⁹⁵ Grants to the University constitute qualifying distributions under IRC § 4942(g), satisfying Foundation payout requirements.²⁹⁶ The University treats grants as ordinary income subject to 21% corporate tax, but this represents tax on University's business income, not tax on endowment investment returns which occurred tax-free at Foundation level.²⁹⁷

**UBIT Concerns**: If the Master Services Agreement is structured as a fee-for-services contract where the Foundation "purchases" educational services from the University rather than making grants, the Foundation may have unrelated business taxable income requiring Form 990-T filing and tax payment at trust rates.²⁹⁸ Properly structured grant arrangements where Foundation retains discretion over grant terms and the University has no enforceable right to receive specific amounts avoid UBIT characterization.²⁹⁹

**Private Foundation Status Risk**: If the Foundation fails to qualify as a Type III supporting organization under IRC § 509(a)(3), it would be classified as a private foundation under § 509(a), subjecting it to:
- 1.39% annual excise tax on investment income (§ 4940);
- 5% minimum distribution requirement (§ 4942);
- Self-dealing prohibitions (§ 4941);
- Excess business holdings limits (§ 4943);
- Jeopardizing investments restrictions (§ 4944);
- Taxable expenditure rules (§ 4945).³⁰⁰

Private foundation status would not prevent the bifurcated structure from functioning, but would impose additional compliance burdens and slightly reduce endowment returns through the 1.39% excise tax.³⁰¹ The Foundation should obtain IRS determination letter confirming § 509(a)(3) supporting organization status as a transaction closing condition.³⁰²

#### D. Alternative Endowment Structures

**1. Complete Endowment Transfer to Separate Foundation**

An alternative structure transfers the entire $468 million endowment to an independent community foundation or newly created grantmaking foundation with no formal relationship to the for-profit University, making competitive grants available to multiple educational institutions including the University.³⁰³ This structure provides maximum assurance of charitable asset preservation and Attorney General approval, but creates significant operational risks:

*Advantages*:
- Eliminates any appearance of for-profit control over charitable assets;
- Simplifies IRS supporting organization compliance;
- May facilitate Attorney General and court approval of conversion;
- Insulates endowment from creditor claims if University enters bankruptcy.³⁰⁴

*Disadvantages*:
- University loses guaranteed access to endowment grants, competing with other institutions for limited foundation funds;
- $19.7M annual grants critical to financial model become uncertain, jeopardizing debt covenant compliance and equity returns;
- Many donor restrictions specifically reference "University" as beneficiary, requiring cy pres court proceedings to redirect to community foundation;
- Destroys $4.9M annual tax benefit from maintaining tax-exempt investment income.³⁰⁵

This structure is appropriate only if the bifurcated foundation approach fails to obtain Attorney General approval, serving as fallback option preserving conversion feasibility at the cost of significant economic value destruction.³⁰⁶

**2. Endowment Retained by For-Profit University**

Retaining the $468 million endowment within the for-profit University's balance sheet avoids structural complexity and maintains unified control, but creates multiple legal barriers:

*Tax Consequences*: Endowment investment income becomes fully taxable at combined 25.9% federal/state rate, reducing after-tax investment returns from 7.2% to 5.3% annually and cutting available spending from $19.7M to $14.6M, creating additional $5.1M annual operating deficit.³⁰⁷

*Charitable Trust Violations*: Donor restrictions requiring endowment principal preservation in perpetuity to fund scholarships and academic programs conflict with for-profit imperative to maximize returns to equity investors, risking Attorney General enforcement for charitable trust breach.³⁰⁸

*UPMIFA Compliance Difficulty*: For-profit board of directors owes fiduciary duties to shareholders to maximize profits, creating structural conflict with UPMIFA duties to preserve donor-restricted endowments and limit spending to prudent rates, making UPMIFA compliance practically impossible.³⁰⁹

*Attorney General Approval Unlikely*: Indiana Attorney General 2018 guidance strongly suggests that for-profit retention of significant donor-restricted endowments will not receive approval absent extraordinary circumstances, treating endowment transfer to separate charitable entity as prerequisite for conversion approval.³¹⁰

**Conclusion**: Retaining endowment within for-profit structure is legally and economically infeasible, leaving bifurcated foundation as the sole viable option.³¹¹

#### E. Endowment Risk Quantification

| Risk Factor | Probability | Downside Impact | Mitigation Strategy | Residual Risk |
|-------------|-------------|-----------------|---------------------|---------------|
| Attorney General refuses bifurcated foundation approval | 25% | Transaction termination | Engage AG counsel pre-signing; obtain informal approval | $195M transaction value (25% × $780M deal) |
| IRS denies § 509(a)(3) supporting organization status | 15% | $650K annual tax cost (1.39% excise tax on $468M) | Obtain determination letter as closing condition | $98K annual (15% × $650K) |
| Foundation Board reduces grants below $19.7M target | 35% | $5M-$10M annual revenue shortfall | Master Services Agreement minimum grant provisions | $1.75M-$3.5M (35% × $5M-$10M range) |
| Court denies charitable trust conversion petition | 20% | Transaction termination; $12M deal break costs | Robust valuation, independent financial advisor, guardian ad litem engagement | $2.4M (20% × $12M) |
| Underwater endowment funds trigger UPMIFA violations | 40% | Attorney General enforcement; grant restrictions | Written policy documenting prudent spending justification | $800K legal/compliance (40% × $2M exposure) |
| **AGGREGATE ENDOWMENT RISK** | — | **$200M+ worst case** | **Robust structure + AG engagement** | **$5M-$8M annual + $49M-$195M transaction risk** |

**CROSS-SECTION IMPACT**: Endowment structure directly affects:
- **Executive Summary (Transaction Recommendation)**: Endowment preservation is the **second-largest transaction risk** after tax implications, requiring bifurcated foundation structure as non-negotiable closing condition.³¹²
- **Purchase Agreement Article 7.5 (Endowment Segregation Covenant)**: Requires Target to establish Foundation entity, transfer endowment assets, obtain IRS determination letter, execute Master Services Agreement, and obtain Indiana Attorney General written approval—all as closing conditions precedent.³¹³
- **Article 8.3 (Fiduciary Duty Indemnification)**: Seller board members provide indemnification for pre-closing breaches of fiduciary duties related to endowment management, UPMIFA violations, and charitable trust compliance failures, with 10-year survival and $50M cap (approximately 11% of endowment value).³¹⁴
